Singletary v. Van Meter


No. 89,325


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


708 So. 2d 266; 1998 Fla. LEXIS 296; 23 Fla. L. Weekly S 118



February 26, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected March 12, 1998. Rehearing Denied April 2, 1998. Released for Publication April 2, 1998.


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the District Court of Appeal - Statutory or Constitutional Invalidity First District - Case No. 96-57 (Leon County).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed in part, reversed in part.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the decision of the District Court of Appeal (Florida) that reversed a dismissal of appellee inmate's mandamus relief and held that the statute of limitations under Fla. Stat. ch. 95.11(8) was an unconstitutional violation of the doctrine of separation of powers.


OVERVIEW: Appellee inmate was transferred from one Florida prison to another. Appellee claimed that the department of corrections (DOC) lost several of his books during the move. The DOC conducted an investigation and reimbursed him. Subsequently, prison officials discovered one of the books in appellee's possession and charged him with lying to prison staff. The prison superintendent and Secretary of the department of corrections denied appellee's appeals. Appellee filed a petition for mandamus relief in circuit court, but the court dismissed the petition as time-barred under Fla. Stat. ch. 95.11(8). The district court reversed and held that the statute was an unconstitutional violation of the doctrine of separation of powers. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision in that Fla. Stat. ch. 95.11(8) could not bar appellee's petition, but reversed the remainder of the opinion and held that the statute did not violate the seperation of powers doctrine. The court reasoned that all of the operative events in the case took place long before the effective date of the statute.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the district court of appeal that held that appellee's case was not time-barred by the statute at issue. However, the court did reverse the portion of the district court of appeal's decision that held that the statute violated the seperation of powers doctrine.

 

80 of 1586 DOCUMENTS


Hopping v. State


No. 89,515


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


708 So. 2d 263; 1998 Fla. LEXIS 238; 23 Fla. L. Weekly S 95



February 19, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Application for Review of the Decision of the District Court of Appeal - Certified Great Public Importance First District - Case No. 95-1344 (Suwannee County).



DISPOSITION: Answered certified question in the affirmative and quash the decision below.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The First District Court of Appeal (Florida) certified a question for review as to whether a criminal sentence that violated double jeopardy principles was an illegal sentence cognizable under Fla. R. Crim. P. 3.800(a). Petitioner, incarcerated after revocation of his probation, requested a sentence correction by filing a motion under Rule 3.800(a).


OVERVIEW: After petitioner's probation was revoked and he was incarcerated, the court had certified to it a question as to whether Fla. R. Crim. P. 3.800(a) was applicable to a criminal sentence that violated double jeopardy principles. The court answered the question in the affirmative, finding that a motion to correct an illegal sentence could be filed at any time and that its subject matter was limited to those sentencing issues that could be resolved as a matter of law without an evidentiary hearing. The court determined that a sentencing court could, at any time, correct an illegal sentence imposed by it, and that petitioner's sentence was illegal as it exceeded the maximum period set forth by law for his offense.


OUTCOME: The court answered the certified question in the affirmative and quashed the decision of the lower court, finding that a criminal sentence that violated double jeopardy principles was an illegal sentence cognizable under the rule for correcting a sentence. Such a motion could be filed at any time and its subject matter was limited to those issues that could be resolved as a matter of law without an evidentiary hearing.

 

81 of 1586 DOCUMENTS


J.A.S. v. State


No. 89,768


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


705 So. 2d 1381; 1998 Fla. LEXIS 85; 23 Fla. L. Weekly S 67



February 5, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Application for Review of the Decision of the District Court of Appeal - Certified Great Public Importance Fifth District - Case Nos. 95-2261 95-2439 (Marion County).



DISPOSITION: Certified question answered in the affirmative to be of great public importance and the Fifth District's decision approved.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Fifth District Court of Appeal (Florida) certified a question concerning the privacy rights of minors and the furtherance of a compelling state interest through the least intrusive means under Fla. Stat. ch. 800.04. The district court had vacated the dismissal of statutory rape charges against petitioners.


OVERVIEW: The district court certified a question involving the application of Fla. Stat. ch. 800.04, which detailed the potential penalty for a minor under the age of 16, and whether the statute furthered a compelling state interest through the least intrusive means. The underlying facts involved the violation of ch. 800.04 by two 15-year old boys who allegedly had "consensual" sexual intercourse with two 12-year old girls. The trial judge found ch. 800.04 unconstitutional as applied to the two boys because it violated their rights to privacy and equal protection. The district court reversed the lower court's decision. The state supreme court found that whatever right of privacy a minor might have in having sexual intercourse with another minor was outweighed by the state's interest in protecting the welfare of other minor children. Minors did not have absolute privacy rights. The court concluded that Fla. Stat. ch. 800.04 was constitutional as applied, because it furthered a compelling state interest through the least intrusive means, and the court therefore approved the lower court's decision.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that statute under which petitioners were charged with statutory rape was constitutional as applied to petitioners because it furthered a compelling state interest through the least intrusive means.

Hudson v. State


No. 85,693


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


708 So. 2d 256; 1998 Fla. LEXIS 87; 23 Fla. L. Weekly S 71



February 5, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] An Appeal and Cross-Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Hillsborough County, Hon. J. Rogers Padgett, Judge - Case No. 86-8613.



DISPOSITION: Hudson's death sentence vacated and remanded to the trial court to consider and prepare, within 120 days of the issuance of this opinion, a detailed sentencing order in compliance with Campbell.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Circuit Court in and for Hillsborough County (Florida), which imposed a death sentence on resentencing, for his convictions of first degree murder, armed burglary, and theft of an automobile. The state cross-appealed, raising three issues concerning the resentencing.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first degree murder, armed robbery, and theft of an automobile, and was sentenced to death. In postconviction proceedings, the circuit court found that appellant's penalty-phase counsel had provided ineffective representation in that he failed to adequately investigate and present extensive mitigation evidence. The court affirmed, a new penalty phase was conducted, and the trial court again imposed a death sentence. On appeal, the court held it could not rule on appellant's issue as to whether the sentence of death was proportionate in light of the resentencing evidence because the trial court's order on the resentencing was so lacking in detail. The trial court's summary analysis of mitigating circumstances, however, plainly did not evaluate in writing the evidence presented or explain the reason for the trial court's weighing of the mitigation evidence. Appellant's sentence was vacated and remanded for the trial court to prepare a detailed analysis in its sentencing order of its evaluation of the mitigation evidence presented. There was no merit in the state's cross-appeal pertaining to the trial court's refusal to apply certain aggravating factors.


OUTCOME: On review of appellant's death sentence, the court found that the trial court's sentencing order did not provide a written evaluation of mitigation evidence presented in the resentencing penalty phase; as such, the sentence was vacated and remanded for the trial court to prepare a more detailed analysis in its sentencing order. The state's cross-appeal as to the trial court's refusal to apply aggravating factors was denied for lack of merit.

 

83 of 1586 DOCUMENTS


Richardson v. State


No. 86,011


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


706 So. 2d 1349; 1998 Fla. LEXIS 82; 23 Fla. L. Weekly S 54



January 29, 1998, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] An Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Volusia County, Hon. Kim C. Hammond, Judge - Case No. 92-30089 CFAES.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment from the Circuit Court in and for Volusia County (Florida), which, after his confession, convicted defendant of first-degree murder and sentenced him to death.


OVERVIEW: The main evidence against defendant was a police officer's testimony that defendant confessed to the murder. There was no dispute defendant was involved in plea negotiations and that the police officer was acting as a negotiator between defendant and the state attorney prosecuting the case. At the time defendant confessed there was an agreement already signed by the state attorney providing defendant would be spared the death penalty if he confessed. The court reversed and remanded defendant's conviction because the confession was obtained during plea negotiations in violation of Fla. R. Crim. P. 3.172(h). It was clear defendant exhibited an actual subjective expectation to negotiate a plea at the time of the discussion because the state had already presented a signed written agreement to defendant. Defendant's expectation was reasonable because it was obvious the state desired to negotiate a deal. It was clear that the officer was negotiating for the state and defendant for himself. The negotiations did not cease when defendant confessed because defendant was told numerous times that the state would negotiate if he gave a statement.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the lower court's murder conviction, finding defendant's confession had to be suppressed because it was obtained during plea negotiations. Defendant's expectation that he was arranging a plea agreement was reasonable because the state brought to the negotiation a signed written agreement for defendant to sign.

Lowrey v. State


No. 89,371


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


705 So. 2d 1367; 1998 Fla. LEXIS 9; 23 Fla. L. Weekly S 27



January 15, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Counsel Corrected January 21, 1998.


PRIOR HISTORY: Application for Review of the Decision of the District Court of Appeal - Certified Great Public Importance First District - Case No. 95-2267 (Columbia County).



DISPOSITION: Certified question answered in the qualified negative, decision of the district court quashed, and remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The District Court of Appeals, First District (Florida), certified a question to determine if defendant was entitled to a new trial without proving harm based on the fact that a juror at his trial was under criminal prosecution at the time and had failed to reveal it.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of carrying a concealed weapon. After the trial, he learned that one of the jurors was under criminal prosecution by the same prosecutor's office and that he had failed to reveal it. Defendant appealed. The district court certified the question as to whether defendant was required to prove harm. The court agreed with the concerns articulated by the district court and answered the certified question with a qualified no, holding that, where it was not revealed to a defendant that a juror was under prosecution by the same office that was prosecuting him, inherent prejudice to the defendant was presumed and defendant was entitled to a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court ordered a new trial finding inherent prejudice where there was a juror who was under prosecution by the same office prosecuting defendant.

Ilkanic v. City of Fort Lauderdale


No. 89,792


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


705 So. 2d 1371; 1998 Fla. LEXIS 37; 23 Fla. L. Weekly S 25



January 15, 1998, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected February 4, 1998.


PRIOR HISTORY: Application for Review of the Decision of the District Court of Appeal - Certified Direct Conflict of Decisions Fourth District - Case No. 95-1679 (Broward County).



DISPOSITION: Decision of the court approved.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review of a judgement rendered by the Fourth District Court of Appeals (Florida) that reversed a judgment finding that a portion of the Civil Restitution Lien and Crime Victims' Remedy Act, Fla. Stat. ch. 960.293(2)(b), was constitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of trespass after warning and was sentenced to forty-five days in jail. Respondent City of Fort Lauderdale moved to impose a lien on petitioner for $ 50 per day for each day of his incarceration, pursuant to Fla. Stat. ch. 960.293(2)(b), which was part of the Civil Restitution Lien and Crime Victims' Remedy Act (the Act). The county court found the Act unconstitutional because it imposed excessive fines and violated due process and equal protection clauses of the Florida and United States Constitutions. The Fourth District Court of Appeal reversed the lower court and found the Act constitutional. Petitioner sought review of the judgment. Petitioner contended that the flat per diem charge violated his substantive due process rights. The court affirmed the judgment of the district court. Applying the test for determining substantive due process, the court found that the Act was not unconstitutional because the per diem charge on convicted offenders clearly related to a permissive legislative objection of reimbursing the public for the cost expended in incarcerating the offenders.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision that reversed a judgment finding the Civil Restitution Lien and Crime Victims' Remedy Act unconstitutional because the Act of imposing per diem charges on convicted offenders related to the permissive legislative objective of reimbursing the public for incarceration.

Kormondy v. State


No. 84,709


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


703 So. 2d 454; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 2188; 23 Fla. L. Weekly S 7



December 24, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Released for Publication December 24, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Escambia County, John P. Kuder, Judge - Case No. 93-3302-E. Cir. Ct. Case No. 93-3302-E.

Original Opinion of October 9, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Fla. LEXIS 1517.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment and sentence of death, by the Circuit Court in and for Escambia County (Florida) convicting appellant of first-degree felony murder, armed sexual battery, burglary of a dwelling with an assault, an intent to commit a theft, and armed robbery.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder and other violent charges. Appellant and his cohorts forced their way into the home of the victims, burglarized the home, raped the wife, and killed the husband by shooting him in the back of the head. Appellant challenged the conviction and death-sentence arguing, inter alia, that the killing was not premeditated and that impermissible evidence was introduced in the penalty phase of the proceedings. The court agreed with appellant that there was insufficient evidence to support a finding of premeditation to the exclusion of all other theories because the gun belonged to the victim and appellant was unfamiliar with the weapon. Nevertheless, the court upheld the first-degree murder conviction. The court also agreed with appellant that the testimony regarding appellant's threat to kill the wife because she could identify him was impermissibly introduced because it was clearly outside the scope of direct examination and not relevant to the penalty phase of the proceedings. The court therefore affirmed the conviction but vacated the death sentence and remanded for a new penalty phase.


OUTCOME: Appellant's death sentence for first-degree murder and other charges was vacated and remanded for a new penalty phase because the finding of premeditation was in error because the prosecution could not exclude all other theories within a reasonable doubt, and because impermissible irrelevant testimony, outside the scope of direct examination, was introduced during the penalty phase.

San Martin v. State


No. 83,611


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


705 So. 2d 1337; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 2186; 23 Fla. L. Weekly S 1



December 24, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected. Certiorari Denied October 5, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 5193.
Writ of certiorari denied San Martin v. Florida, 525 U.S. 841, 119 S. Ct. 105, 142 L. Ed. 2d 84, 1998 U.S. LEXIS 5193 (1998)
Post-conviction relief denied at San Martin v. State, 2008 Fla. LEXIS 1460 (Fla., Aug. 28, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Dade County, Rodolfo Sorondo, Jr., Judge - Case No. 92-6089-C.
Franqui v. State, 632 So. 2d 1040, 1994 Fla. App. LEXIS 730 (Fla. Dist. Ct. App. 3d Dist., 1994)



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Circuit Court in and for Dade County (Florida), which found him guilty of first-degree murder and sentenced him to death. He was also given life imprisonment on 2 attempted murder charges, 15 years imprisonment on attempted robbery and unlawful firearm possession charges, and 5 years imprisonment on 2 grand theft charges. All sentences were ordered to run consecutively.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to death. He was also found guilty of other charges and the court imposed the following consecutive sentences: life imprisonment for 2 attempted murder charges; 15 years imprisonment for attempted robbery and unlawful firearm possession charges; and 5 years imprisonment for 2 grand theft charges. Appellant challenged the ruling. The court affirmed the trial court judgment after finding no reversible error. The court held that defendant's confrontation clause rights were not violated by the trial court's denial of defendant's motion to sever or by the introduction of a co-defendant's confession implicating appellant. The court held that although admission of that portion of the co-defendant's confession implicating appellant should not have been introduced into evidence, the error was harmless. The court found that appellant's own confession was powerful evidence of his guilt and that the co-defendant's confession merely mirrored appellant's confession. The court noted that there was also corroborating eyewitness testimony and physical evidence that supported the jury's verdict.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court that found appellant guilty of murder and various other charges and imposed a death sentence and consecutive prison sentences was affirmed. The court found that a co-defendant's confession implicating appellant was erroneously introduced into evidence; however, the error was harmless because the confession mirrored appellant's own confession and was corroborated by eyewitness testimony and physical evidence.

Writ of habeas corpus denied Grossman v. Crosby, 359 F. Supp. 2d 1233, 2005 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 1497 (M.D. Fla., 2005)


PRIOR HISTORY: Two Cases Consolidated: An Original Proceeding - Habeas Corpus and An Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Pinellas County, Crockett Farnell, Judge - Case No. 84-11698-CF-ANO.
Grossman v. Florida, 489 U.S. 1071, 109 S. Ct. 1354, 103 L. Ed. 2d 822, 1989 U.S. LEXIS 1314, 57 U.S.L.W. 3588 (1989)



DISPOSITION: Denial of Grossman's rule 3.850 motion affirmed, and petition for writ of habeas corpus denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order from the Circuit Court in and for Pinellas County (Florida), which denied his request for relief under Fla. R. Crim. P. 3.850 to vacate or set aside his conviction and death sentence for first-degree murder. Defendant also petitioned the court for a writ of habeas corpus.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the first-degree murder of a wildlife officer and sentenced to death. After his conviction was affirmed, defendant filed a petition for a writ of habeas corpus. He also filed a Fla. R. Crim. P. 3.850 motion with the lower court. That motion was denied and he appealed. Addressing the rule 3.850 motion, the court held that trial counsel did not provide ineffective assistance of counsel during the penalty phase. Counsel called three mitigating witnesses in addition to appellant's mother, all of whom had close contact with defendant. The court also found no merit to defendant's ineffective assistance of appellate counsel claim made in his petition for writ of habeas corpus. Appellate counsel's failure to object to the sentencing order as being insufficiently specific was proper because the order comported with the law in effect at that time. Finding his remaining claims in both pleadings to be meritless or procedurally barred, the court affirmed the denial of his rule 3.850 motion and denied his petition for writ of habeas corpus.


OUTCOME: Defendant's motion to vacate or set aside his conviction and death sentence and his habeas corpus petition were denied. Trial counsel provided effective assistance at the penalty phase by presenting four mitigating witnesses, his appellate counsel did not provide ineffective assistance for failing to object to the sentencing order because the order complied with the law, and all remaining arguments were meritless or procedurally barred.

Valle v. State

SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


705 So. 2d 1331; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 2094; 22 Fla. L. Weekly S 751

OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was sentenced to death for first-degree murder, sought postconviction relief under Fla. R. Crim. P. 3.850. Defendant argued that his counsel was ineffective for failing to move for the disqualification of the judge at his resentencing. Finally, the court agreed with the trial court that other claims in defendant's motion below were procedurally barred.


OUTCOME: The court reversed in part and remanded for an evidentiary hearing, and as to all other issues, affirmed the decision of the trial court because the allegations in defendant's motion regarding judge's conduct and counsel's failure to move for disqualification of the judge were sufficient to warrant an evidentiary hearing on this issue.

Mendyk v. State


No. 88,062


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


707 So. 2d 320; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 2096; 22 Fla. L. Weekly S 749



December 11, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Denied March 24, 1998. Released for Publication March 24, 1998.

DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Circuit Court for Hernando County (Florida) denying his motion for post-conviction relief filed pursuant to Fla. R. Crim. P. 3.850. Appellant contended that the trial court erred in denying him an evidentiary hearing or the right to take depositions on his public records claim, which was based solely on the affidavits of records custodians submitted by the state.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of first-degree murder, two counts of sexual battery, and one count of kidnapping and was sentenced to death. His convictions and sentence were affirmed on direct appeal. Appellant was denied clemency, but his execution was stayed to allow for post-conviction litigation. Appellant challenged a trial court judgment denying his motion for relief pursuant to Fla. R. Crim. P. 3.850 and argued that the trial court erred in denying him an evidentiary hearing or the right to take depositions on his public records claim, which was based solely on the affidavits of records custodians submitted by the state. The appeals court affirmed. The court agreed that appellant should have been allowed to explore the existence of a missing, unedited videotape of the murder scene but held that the trial court's failure to allow appellant to cross-examine or depose the affiants was harmless. Moreover, the court ruled that because appellant confessed to the murder several times and his codefendant testified against him at trial, even if the tape was available, there was no possibility that it would contain any information that could form the basis for a claim under rule 3.850.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the judgment of the trial court denying appellant's motion for postconviction relief and held that that even if missing evidence was available, there was no possibility that it would contain any information which could form the basis for a claim for post-conviction relief.

Jackson v. State

SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


704 So. 2d 500; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 1853; 22 Fla. L. Weekly S 690

PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Duval County, Donald R. Moran, Chief Judge. Case No. 83-4600 CF.



DISPOSITION: Jackson's sentence vacated and case remanded with directions.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prisoner challenged her conviction and sentence from the Circuit Court in and for Duval County (Florida) for first-degree murder of a police officer.


OVERVIEW: Appellant prisoner was convicted of first-degree murder and sentenced to death. Prisoner contended that the trial court erred when it held that the murder was cold, calculated, and premeditated. The court held that based on the record, the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it found that the murder was cold, calculated, and premeditated. Prisoner also contended that the trial court failed to consider, find, and weigh unrefuted statutory and nonstatutory mitigation factor. The court found that the trial court's failure to have expressly evaluated each mitigating factor precluded meaningful review of this issue. The court noted that the trial court's order summarily disposed of the statutory and nonstatutory mitigators without explanation or reference to testimony. The court remanded the case to the trial court. The court directed the trial court to reweigh the aggravating and mitigating circumstances, and instructed the trial court that if it again determined that death was the appropriate penalty, that the trial court must prepare a sentencing order that expressly discussed and weighed the evidence offered in mitigation.


OUTCOME: Appellant prisoner's sentence was vacated and remanded with directions to the trial court to reweigh the aggravating and mitigating circumstances and resentence prisoner because the trial court failed to adequately explain its evaluation of the mitigating factors.

 

94 of 1586 DOCUMENTS


Wood v. Eli Lilly & Co.


No. 90,001


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


701 So. 2d 344; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 1815; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P15,101; 22 Fla. L. Weekly S 680



October 30, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Certified Question of Law from the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit - Case No. 95-4924.



DISPOSITION: Certified question answered by holding that the statute of limitations for DES negligence actions begins to run on the date the plaintiff knew or should have known of his or her injury rather than on the date that Conley was decided. Record returned to the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sisters sought review of a trial court's grant of appellee manufacturers' motion for summary judgment on appellants' negligence claims, on the basis that they were time barred by a four year statute of limitations; the Eleventh Circuit Court of Appeal (Florida) certified a question for review. Plaintiff brother and appellant sisters had sued appellees for injuries allegedly sustained when their mother ingested appellees' drug.


OVERVIEW: Appellant sisters and plaintiff brother alleged they were injured as a result of their exposure to appellee manufacturers' drug when their mother ingested it while she was pregnant. Appellants were informed of their possible injuries, but did not bring their action within four years. The trial court granted appellee's motion for summary judgment on the grounds that appellants' action was barred by the statute of limitations. Appellants contended their cause of action did not accrue until a case was decided that allowed them to assert the market-share alternative theory of liability because they were unable to identify the manufacturers of the drug ingested by their mother. The lower court certified for review the question of when the cause of action accrued. The state supreme court held that there was nothing in the case law construing the statute of limitations for negligence actions that supported the conclusion that the running of the statute commenced when a new theory of liability was adopted or even when a new cause of action was created. Rather, the statute of limitations for appellants' cause of action began to run when appellants knew or should have known of their injury.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court answered the lower court's certified question regarding the commencement of the statute of limitations in appellants' action in the negative. The state supreme court held that the statute of limitations for appellants' negligence action began to run on the date appellants knew or should have known of their injury, rather than on the date that a case allowing them to proceed under a market share of liability was decided.

Jimenez v. State


No. 85,014


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


703 So. 2d 437; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 1816; 22 Fla. L. Weekly S 685



October 30, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Corrected. Rehearing Denied December 29, 1997. Released Publication December 29, 1997. Certiorari Denied May 18, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 3187.
Writ of certiorari denied Jimenez v. Florida, 523 U.S. 1123, 118 S. Ct. 1806, 140 L. Ed. 2d 945, 1998 U.S. LEXIS 3187 (1998)
Post-conviction relief denied at Jimenez v. State, 810 So. 2d 511, 2001 Fla. LEXIS 1906 (Fla., 2001)
Writ of habeas corpus denied, Motion denied by, As moot Jimenez v. Crosby, 2006 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 101502 (S.D. Fla., Jan. 27, 2006)
Post-conviction relief denied at Jimenez v. State, 2014 Fla. LEXIS 3217 (Fla., Oct. 29, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Dade County, Leslie Rothenberg, Judge - Case No. 92-34156.



DISPOSITION: Convictions and sentence of death affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Circuit Court, in and for Dade County (Florida), that convicted appellant of burglary with an assault and battery in an occupied dwelling and first degree murder. Furthermore, appellant sought review of the judgment that sentenced appellant to death.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of burglary with an assault and battery in an occupied dwelling and first degree murder. A death sentence was imposed. Upon review, the court held that denial of appellant's request to discharge an appointed co-counsel was not improper because appellant had no right to co-counsel. Furthermore, it was not error for appellant to be absent from two sidebar conferences during the jury selection process because appellant was present in the courtroom. In addition, there was sufficient evidence to support the first degree murder and burglary convictions based on premeditation or felony murder. The court held that a trier of fact could have reasonably found proof of the victim's withdrawal of consent to enter victim's apartment beyond a reasonable doubt. There was no reasonable hypothesis of evidence. Finally, the trial court applied the right rule of law regarding aggravators, competent substantial evidence supported the findings of the aggravators, the trial court did not abuse its discretion by assigning minimal weight to mitigating factors, and the death sentence was held to be proportionate. Therefore, the convictions and sentence were affirmed.


OUTCOME: The judgment that convicted appellant of burglary with an assault and battery in an occupied dwelling and first degree murder and that sentenced appellant to death was affirmed, because there was sufficient evidence to support the convictions, the trial court properly considered the aggravators, the trial court did not abuse its discretion by assigning minimal weight to mitigating factors, and the death sentence was proportionate to the crime.

 

96 of 1586 DOCUMENTS


Chandler v. State


No. 84,812


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


702 So. 2d 186; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 1529; 22 Fla. L. Weekly S 649



October 16, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Denied December 11, 1197. Released Publication December 11, 1197. Certiorari Denied April 20, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 2682.
Writ of certiorari denied Chandler v. Florida, 523 U.S. 1083, 118 S. Ct. 1535, 140 L. Ed. 2d 685, 1998 U.S. LEXIS 2682 (1998)
Post-conviction relief denied at Chandler v. State, 848 So. 2d 1031, 2003 Fla. LEXIS 525 (Fla., 2003)
Post-conviction relief denied at Chandler v. State, 2011 Fla. LEXIS 2655 (Fla., Nov. 7, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Pinellas County, Susan F. Schaeffer, Judge - Case No. CRC92-17438-CFANO-B.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant sought review of convictions for three counts of first degree murder and three death sentences imposed by the Circuit Court in and for Pinellas County (Florida), stemming from the rapes and murders of a female tourist and her two daughters.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged three death sentences imposed after he was convicted of luring a female tourist and her two daughters into a boat, raping and murdering them, and dumping their bodies at sea. On appeal, the court held that evidence showing that defendant raped another female tourist at sea one month earlier was properly admitted as a prior bad act under Fla. Stat. Ann. § 90.404(2)(a) to prove identity because pervasive similarities existed in the modus operandi of the crimes. The other act evidence was also relevant to show defendant's plan, scheme, intent, and motive to commit violence on women tourists. The admission of this other act evidence did not, in effect, force defendant to repeatedly invoke his U. S. Const. amend. V right to remain silent on the witness stand. Admission of defendant's daughter's prior consistent statement, that defendant told her he was wanted for committing the murders, was relevant to impeach defendant's assertion that he told his daughter he was innocent. During the penalty phase, defendant validly waived presentation of mitigating evidence showing that he suffered an abusive childhood. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's three murder convictions and three death sentences, holding that other act evidence was properly admitted to prove identity, scheme, plan, and motive and that admission of a prior consistent statement by defendant's daughter was relevant to impeach defendant's testimony that he had told his daughter that he was innocent of the murders.

Hoskins v. State


No. 84,737


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


702 So. 2d 202; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 1528; 22 Fla. L. Weekly S 643



October 16, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing Denied December 9, 1997. Released for Publication December 9, 1997.


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal and Cross-Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Brevard County, Harry Stein, Judge - Case No. 92-17795-CF-A.



DISPOSITION: Convictions and sentences affirmed except the sentence of death. Remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal defendant sought review of his convictions in the Circuit Court in and for Brevard County (Florida), for first degree murder, burglary of a dwelling, sexual battery with physical force, kidnapping, and robbery. Defendant also challenged the death sentence imposed for the first degree murder conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged the trial court's judgment convicting him of multiple felonies, including first degree murder, sexual battery with physical force, kidnapping, burglary, and robbery as well as the death sentence imposed. Defendant, who had an IQ of 71, brutally raped and battered his 81-year-old neighbor, dumped her body into the trunk of her car, and drove to Georgia where he buried her. On appeal, the court held that the jurors represented a fair cross-section of the community even though, pursuant to a standing court order, the county clerk excused members of the venire in violation of Fla. Stat. Ann. § 40.013(5) (1995). Because defendant did not timely challenge the clerk's practice, this issue was not preserved for appeal. The aggravating factor of premeditation did not exist. However, the trial court erred in refusing to grant defendant's motion seeking transport to a hospital to have a brain scan performed so that a defense specialist could evaluate the degree of defendant's mental impairment, a statutory mitigating factor under Fla. Stat. Ann. § 921.141 (1995). The court remanded, ordering a brain scan and, possibly, a new penalty phase.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for, inter alia, first degree murder but remanded so that defendant could be transported to a hospital for a brain scan to explore whether imposition of the death sentence might be mitigated by virtue of poor impulse control caused by brain damage. If medical evidence so adduced supported the mitigating factor, the court ordered a new penalty phase.

 

98 of 1586 DOCUMENTS


Kormondy v. State


No. 84,709


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


1997 Fla. LEXIS 1517; 22 Fla. L. Weekly S 635



October 9, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [*1] Opinion Reissued on Grant of Rehearing of December 24, 1997, Reported at: 1997 Fla. LEXIS 2188.


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Escambia County, John P. Kuder, Judge - Case No. 93-3302-E.



DISPOSITION: Kormondy's convictions affirmed for one count of first-degree felony murder, three counts of armed sexual battery, one count of burglary of a dwelling with an assault and an intent to commit a theft, and one count of armed robbery. However, his sentence of death vacated and remanded for a new penalty-phase proceeding before a new jury.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the Circuit Court, Escambia County (Florida), where he was convicted of first degree felony murder, armed sexual battery, burglary of a dwelling with an assault and intent to commit a theft, and armed robbery. Defendant claimed the trial court erred at the penalty phase of his trial by permitting testimony indicating intent by defendant to commit future killings if he got out of jail.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of one court of first degree felony murder, three counts of armed sexual battery, one count of burglary of a dwelling with an assault and intent to commit a theft, and one count of armed robbery. The trial judge imposed the sentence of death for the murder. The appellate court affirmed defendant's convictions but vacated his sentence of death, holding that the trial court erred in the penalty phase of the trial in admitting bad character evidence in the form of nonstatutory aggravating circumstances. The trial court's reversible error was allowing a witness to testify, on cross-examination by the state, to a statement defendant allegedly made to a cellmate in jail, after the murder, that defendant would kill the wife of the murder victim if he ever got out of jail. The testimony was prejudicial because it indicated that defendant would kill again. The testimony was inflammatory and could have unduly influenced the penalty-phase jury. The manner in which the cross-examination was conducted constituted an impermissible, nonstatutory aggravating circumstance.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed the convictions of defendant for first degree felony murder, armed sexual battery, burglary of a dwelling with an assault and intent to commit a theft, and armed robbery but vacated his sentence of death because, at the penalty phase, the trial court allowed testimony of an impermissible, nonstatutory aggravating circumstance. The testimony indicated that defendant would kill again if released from jail.

Monlyn v. State


No. 82,779


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


705 So. 2d 1; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 1520; 22 Fla. L. Weekly S 631



October 9, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Revised on Denial of Rehearing January 22, 1998. Certiorari Denied June 26, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 4359.
Corrected by, Rehearing denied by, 01/22/1998
Post-conviction relief denied at, Writ of habeas corpus denied Monlyn v. State, 2004 Fla. LEXIS 2170 (Fla., Dec. 2, 2004)


PRIOR HISTORY: An Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Madison County, E. Vernon Douglas, Judge - Case No. 92-147CF.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment from the Circuit Court in and for Madison County (Florida), which convicted him of first-degree murder and other crimes and the sentenced him to death. Appellant's major assignments of error related to the trial court's handling of testimony and cross-examination, and refusal to give certain jury instructions.


OVERVIEW: The court found no abuse of discretion in the trial court's allowance of testimony, found no error in the failure to give certain instructions, and affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence. The court held that it was proper for the medical examiner to offer his opinion that the victim was still alive after appellant had bound and gagged him, and thus perhaps received a fatal wound in that condition. The court held that appellant had opened the door to cross-examination about whether he bit the victim, and held that inquiry into appellant's understanding of the victim's condition when appellant left him was proper. The court held that appellant's statement, made the day before he escaped from prison, that he was going to kill the victim, was admissible under Fla. Stat. ch. 90.803(3)(a)(2) (1995) to prove appellant's conduct. The court ruled that it was not necessary to give a circumstantial evidence instruction, and that the standard instructions on reasonable doubt and heinous, atrocious, or cruel aggravating circumstances were constitutional. The court held that there was no improper doubling of aggravating factors, since both robbery and kidnapping had been proved.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence for first-degree murder. The court found no error in the trial court's allowance of expert opinion testimony or cross-examination, ruled that certain jury instructions had been properly refused or given, and held that sufficient independent aggravating factors existed so that the jury did not impermissibly double the aggravating factors of robbery and pecuniary gain.

 

100 of 1586 DOCUMENTS


L.B. v. State


No. 89,424


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


700 So. 2d 370; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 1512; 22 Fla. L. Weekly S 609



October 2, 1997, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] An Appeal from the District Court of Appeal Statutory or Constitutional Invalidity. Second District - Case No. 95-01693.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded this case to the Second District for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After appellant student's conviction for possession of a weapon on school property in violation of Fla. Stat. ch. 790.001(13), the court reviewed the subsequent decision of the Florida District Court of Appeals which declared that statute unconstitutionally vague because the term "pocketknife" was not defined with sufficient definiteness.


OVERVIEW: After appellant student's conviction for possession of a weapon on school property in violation of Fla. Stat. ch. 790.001(13), the court reviewed the subsequent decision which declared that statute unconstitutionally vague because the term "pocketknife" was not defined with sufficient definiteness. Specifically, the common pocketknife exception to the statute that prohibited possessing a weapon on school property was found to be void for vagueness, because the term pocketknife was not defined with sufficient definiteness. The court reversed the judgment, holding that in the vast majority of cases it would be evident whether one's particular knife was a common pocketknife, and therefore that term was not void for vagueness. Also, all doubts as to the validity of a statute were to be resolved in favor of constitutionality where reasonably possible, and it was reasonable that most people would understand the definition of a common pocketknife. Finally, appellant's conviction under the statute was vacated because the knife she possessed on school property was a common pocketknife and therefore the pocketknife exception to the statute applied.


OUTCOME: The judgment holding the weapons statute unconstitutionally vague was reversed and the appellant's conviction was vacated because the term "pocketknife" was not void for vagueness, and the knife appellant carried fell into the common definition of a pocketknife, which was excluded from the weapons statute.

Phillips v. State


No. 83,731


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


705 So. 2d 1320; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 1458; 22 Fla. L. Weekly S 607



September 25, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Writ of certiorari denied Phillips v. Florida, 525 U.S. 880, 119 S. Ct. 187, 142 L. Ed. 2d 152, 1998 U.S. LEXIS 5750 (1998)
Writ of habeas corpus denied Phillips v. State, 894 So. 2d 28, 2004 Fla. LEXIS 1808 (Fla., 2004)
Related proceeding at Phillips v. State, 2008 Fla. LEXIS 442 (Fla., Mar. 20, 2008)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] An Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Dade County, Arthur I. Snyder, Judge - Case No. 83-435.
Phillips v. Florida, 509 U.S. 908, 113 S. Ct. 3005, 125 L. Ed. 2d 697, 1993 U.S. LEXIS 4314 (1993)



DISPOSITION: The trial court's sentence of death affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the order from the Circuit Court, Dade County (Florida), sentencing him to death.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of murdering his parole supervisor and was sentenced to death. On remand, the trial court reimposed the death sentence finding the following aggravating factors: (1) defendant was under a sentence of imprisonment at the time of the murder; (2) defendant had prior violent felony convictions; (3) the murder was committed to disrupt or hinder the lawful exercise of a governmental function; and (4) the murder was cold, calculated, and premeditated. Defendant appealed alleging that the trial court improperly influenced the jury to return a death verdict. Defendant also alleged that the disrupt/hinder aggravator was improperly submitted to the jury. The court found that the jury was never informed of defendant's previous death sentence and was not prejudiced by the trial court's comment. The court also determined that defendant never objected to the actual jury instruction at trial so it was not preserved for review. The disrupt/hinder aggravating factor was properly applied because the homicide was committed to disrupt or hinder governmental function. The court affirmed defendant's death sentence because his claims were without merit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's sentence of death because the court found that the trial court properly handled the jury. The court noted that defendant never objected to the Allen charge given by the trial court, and the disrupt/hinder-aggravating factor was present because defendant murdered his parole officer to disrupt the governmental function of supervising his parole.

Blanco v. State


No. 85,118


SUPREME COURT OF FLORIDA


706 So. 2d 7; 1997 Fla. LEXIS 1435; 22 Fla. L. Weekly S 575



September 18, 1997, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Certiorari Denied October 5, 1998, Reported at: 1998 U.S. LEXIS 5126.
Post-conviction relief denied at Blanco v. State, 702 So. 2d 1250, 1997 Fla. LEXIS 1434 (Fla., 1997)
Writ of certiorari denied Blanco v. Florida, 525 U.S. 837, 119 S. Ct. 96, 142 L. Ed. 2d 76, 1998 U.S. LEXIS 5126 (1998)
Post-conviction relief denied at Blanco v. State, 2007 Fla. LEXIS 662 (Fla., Apr. 12, 2007)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] An Appeal from the Circuit Court in and for Broward County, Barry E. Goldstein, Judge - Case No. 82-453CF10A.
Blanco v. State, 475 So. 2d 693, 1985 Fla. LEXIS 3592 (Fla., 1985)



DISPOSITION: The death sentence affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate sought review of his death penalty sentence for first-degree murder from the Circuit Court in and for Broward County (Florida).


OVERVIEW: Appellant inmate was convicted of first-degree murder and was sentenced to death. Appellant's sentence was reversed but reinstated at his resentencing based on the testimony of witnesses and two mental health experts. Appellant claimed that the court erred in refusing to allow the mental health expert of his choice to testify. The appellate court affirmed and held that while the court-hired psychiatrist may not have been appellant's first choice, he nonetheless was appellant's expert of choice in that he was approved by appellant prior to appointment and was given high marks before testifying. The fact that the psychiatrist's testimony did not live up to appellant's expectations was not categorized as a trial court error.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's death sentence because it found that appellant's conviction for first-degree murder was adequately supported in the record and the sentence of death was proportionate to the crime.
